Markets
Yuan Rising as China Economy Shows Signs of Improvement; Gained 1.5% Against U.S. Dollar Since This Year's Low in April
585 words
31 July 2014
06:27
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

China's yuan has rebounded from an unprecedented tumble earlier in the year, finishing July with a third monthly advance against the U.S. dollar and traders saying it is primed for further gains amid signs that the world's second-largest economy is strengthening.

The Chinese currency has risen 1.5% against the dollar since it hit this year's low on April 30. On Thursday, the last trading day of the month, the dollar was buying 6.1747 yuan. The yuan is still down 2% for the year because of heavy losses between January and April, largely attributable to Beijing's efforts to shake out speculators betting on gains.

The recent strength comes as data rolls in showing better growth this month in manufacturing and the property market, deemed to be critical to the economy's health, also showing signs of life. That is a turnaround from the start of the year when fears grew over a potential hard landing for the economy and rising credit worries.

"As the signs of recovery firm up, the authorities should get more comfortable with allowing some [yuan] appreciation again," said Linan Liu, a Deutsche Bank strategist.

Huang Yi, a senior trader at China Guangfa Bank that is partly owned by Citigroup Inc., said the yuan may climb as much as 0.4%, with the dollar falling to 6.15 yuan in the third quarter. A lower number means a stronger yuan.

"China is expected to enjoy a large trade surplus and with more corporate clients coming back to support the yuan," he said.

A trade surplus indicates rising demand for exports and more inflows to China that add to purchases of the yuan. It recovered to $31.6 billion in June, from US$16.9 billion in the first quarter, the smallest quarterly surplus in two years. The surplus was expected to end July at $28.9 billion, according to information provider Wind Information Co.

The yuan, which is still tightly controlled, has been rising even as the central bank sets it weaker, a sign that traders' bets are outweighing authorities' efforts to control the currency. For more than half of the trading days in July, the People's Bank of China set the "central parity rate"—against which the currency can trade 2% above or below in a day—weaker instead of stronger. But in the market, the yuan rose 0.5% for the month.

The International Monetary Fund said in a report Wednesday that the yuan remained moderately undervalued by 5% to 10%, given the large current-account surplus and economic fundamentals.

In the offshore market, where the currency is freely traded, traders say global investors, including hedge funds, are betting again on appreciation.

The yuan's strength has been accompanied by a strong performance of Chinese stocks. The Shanghai Composite Index rose to its highest level in seven months this past week, as investors snapped up big blue chips on the fresh signs of economic recovery and news of an official investigation into a high-profile official, Zhou Yongkang.

A corruption investigation into Mr. Zhou is a positive for the economy, Bank of America analyst Lu Ting said, because it suggests that Communist Party leader Xi Jinping has made significant progress in consolidating power and "the new government can finally focus on economic matters."

Wynne Wang and Fiona Law

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Home-Price Growth Slows Sharply, Case-Shiller Says; Index Says Year-Over-Year Growth Rate Is Lowest Since February 2013
By Kathleen Madigan
285 words
29 July 2014
05:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Home prices across the U.S. slowed sharply to a single-digit pace in May on a year-over-year basis, the slowest rate since February 2013, according to a home-price report.

A survey covering 10 major U.S. cities increased 9.4% in the year ended in May, said the S&P/Case-Shiller Home Price Index survey released Tuesday. The 20-city price index increased 9.3%. That is down from a 10.8% yearly pace in April and much lower than the 9.9% expected by economists.

On an unadjusted basis, the 10-city index and the 20-city composite each increased 1.1% in May over April. Seasonally adjusted, both indexes declined 0.3%.

Past increases in home prices coupled with stagnant wage growth have recently eroded housing affordability. Home sales have struggled in the first half, and early data suggest more problems in the third quarter. The National Association of Realtors reported Monday that pending home sales fell 1.1% in June to stand 7.3% below year-ago levels.

"Housing has been turning in mixed economic numbers in the last few months," said David M. Blitzer, chairman of the index committee at S&PDow Jones Indices. "Prices and sales of existing homes have shown improvement while construction and sales of new homes continue to lag."

Regionally, 18 of 20 cities had slower year-over-year growth in May than in April. San Francisco and San Diego saw their year-over-year figures decelerate by about three percentage points, the report said.

Monday's Markets
Investors Play Waiting Game --- Stocks End Flat Ahead of Fed Meeting, Earnings Reports, Data Later This Week
By Dan Strumpf
820 words
29 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Stocks finished little changed as investors paused ahead of the next Federal Reserve policy meeting and a slew of economic and corporate earnings reports due out this week.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 22.02 points, or 0.1%, to 16982.59. The S&P 500 added 0.57 point, to 1978.91, while the Nasdaq Composite Index shed 4.65 points, or 0.1%, to 4444.91.

Stocks started Monday heading lower, with selling fueled by another in a string of weak reports on housing. The National Association of Realtors said its index of pending home sales fell in June, bucking expectations for a rise and indicating continued softness in the housing market..

The Dow was down as much as 82.85 points in the morning, but recovered as the day wore on. Despite the early move, traders said most investors were sitting tight.

"You can push around markets fairly easy on low volume," said Michael Antonelli, sales trader at Robert W. Baird.

Helping to keep activity restrained Monday was a full slate of news to come this week. On Wednesday, the Fed will announce the results of its latest two-day policy-setting meeting. Investors expect the Fed to continue paring back the bond purchases it has been using to ease monetary policy but make no other significant changes.

Investors are also waiting on the monthly jobs report for July on Friday. A report on second-quarter economic growth is also on tap for Wednesday.

Economists expect the Labor Department to report that 230,000 jobs were added in July, a modest pullback from the 288,000 added in June. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal expect the economy expanded 3% in the second quarter, bouncing back from a 2.9% contraction in the first period.

This week marks one of the busiest for second-quarter earnings. Among companies in the S&P 500, 148 are due to report earnings this week, according to FactSet. So far, the news has been good, with companies in the S&P 500 on track to report earnings growth of 6.4% from a year ago, according to FactSet. That compares with expectations of a 4.9% rise before earnings season started.

"Once they get through earnings, people plan to revisit the names that they like," said Brian Fenske, head of sales trading at brokerage ITG.

In corporate news, Ireland's Ryanair Holdings, reported first-quarter profit soared, prompting it to raise its full-year earnings forecast and promise a special dividend. U.S.-traded shares of the discount airline gained $1.46, or 2.8%, to $54.06.

Family Dollar Stores surged 15.08, or 25%, to 75.74, after news that the discount chain will be acquired by Dollar Tree in a cash-and-stock deal that values the struggling retailer at about $8.5 billion. Dollar Tree rose 65 cents, or 1.2%, to 54.87.

Zillow gained 1.46, or 0.9%, to 160.32, after the provider of online real-estate information reached a deal to buy rival Trulia in a stock-for-stock deal valued at about $2.6 billion. Trulia rose 8.69, or 15%, to 65.04.

Tyson Foods advanced 1.02, or 2.6%, to 40.56, after the food company said fiscal third-quarter profit and sales rose on higher prices and volumes. The company also said it was preparing to close its deal to buy Hillshire Brands and reached a deal to sell chicken operations in Mexico and Brazil.

In Europe, shares gave up early gains, with the Stoxx Europe 600 index falling 0.2%.

Adding to the mood in Europe, political leaders met to put the finishing touches to sanctions against Russia over the conflict in Ukraine.

Frankfurt's DAX index, which is generally sensitive to tension between Moscow and the West due to Germany's close trade and energy links with Russia, slid 0.5%. Russian assets also suffered. Moscow's Micex index shed 1.9% and the ruble weakened against the dollar.

In Asia, Japan's Nikkei Stock Average hit a six-month high, rising 0.5%, buoyed by solid earnings from some industrial companies. Chinese shares hit a seven-month high on signs of a recovering economy and expectations that Beijing will accelerate overhauls in the banking sector. The Shanghai Composite Index rose 2.4%.

In commodities markets, crude-oil prices fell 0.4%, to $101.67 a barrel. Gold added less than 0.1%, to $1,303.30 a troy ounce. The dollar fell against the euro and edged up slightly versus the yen. The yield on the 10-year Treasury note edged higher to 2.492%, as prices fell.

Abreast of the Market
Soft Housing Data Crimp U.S. Stocks
By Dan Strumpf
647 words
29 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
21
English
Copyright © 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

U.S. stocks slipped Monday following a disappointing reading on the housing market, while investors looked ahead to a busy week of data and corporate earnings.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average lost 32 points, or 0.2%, to 16928 in midday trading. The S&P 500 index shed five points, or 0.2%, to 1974, while the Nasdaq Composite Index eased 19 points, or 0.4%, to 4431.

Major indexes slid after a reading on pending home sales came in weaker than expected, the latest sign that the U.S. housing market remains weak despite low interest rates. The National Association of Realtors said its index of pending home sales -- reflecting purchases under contract -- fell 1.1% to 102.7 in June, ending three months of gains. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had expected a 0.5% rise.

A read on second-quarter gross domestic product is due Wednesday, while the U.S. government's monthly July jobs report is due on Friday.

"This week is going to be a big test of the economy and the markets," said Mike Binger, senior portfolio manager at Gradient Investments, a $600 million investment firm based in Minneapolis.

Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal expect the economy grew 2.9% in the second quarter. Employers are seen adding 234,000 jobs in July compared with 288,000 added in June.

Despite the disappointing housing number, most investors are of the mind that the economy is steadily improving, said Michael Antonelli, sales trader at Robert W. Baird. "Most institutional clients have come around to the notion that unemployment is continuing to improve and the jobs situation is continuing to improve," he said.

Later this week, the Federal Reserve's policy-making body is due to convene. Investors are also looking out for any hints on when the Fed might raise interest rates, stepping back from an accommodative stance widely believed to have helped fuel stocks' multiyear rally.

Shares of small companies fell. The Russell 2000 small-cap index declined nine points, or 0.8%, to 1136.

Ireland's Ryanair Holdings, Europe's largest discount airline, reported first-quarter profit soared, prompting it to raise its full-year profit forecast and promise a special dividend. U.S.-traded shares gained 2.8%.

Family Dollar Stores jumped 24% after news that the discount chain will be uired by Dollar Tree in a cash-and-stock deal that values the struggling retailer at about $8.5 billion. Shares of Dollar Tree advanced 3.1%.

Zillow fell 2.2% after the provider of online real-estate information reached a deal to buy rival Trulia in a stock-for-stock deal valued at about $2.6 billion. Trulia shares rose 12%.

Tyson Foods added 3.8% after the food giant said fiscal third-quarter profit and sales rose on higher prices and volumes. The company also said it was preparing to close its deal to buy Hillshire Brands and reached a deal to sell chicken operations in Mexico and Brazil.

European shares declined, with the Stoxx Europe 600 shedding 0.2% to 341.34. Poor U.S. data and concerns over looming sanctions against Russia overshadowed largely upbeat corporate earnings.

Russian assets also retreated. Moscow's Micex index tumbled 1.9. Bankia, Spain's largest nationalized bank, rose 1.3% after reporting that its quarterly net profit nearly doubled as loan losses fell.

French dairy group Danone climbed 0.9% on news it is in talks to sell its medical nutrition business to U.S. firm Hospira, in a deal that could be valued at about $5 billion. Hospira shares rose 2%.

Aberdeen Asset Management dropped 5.3% after the U.K. firm posted a decline in assets under management.

US
Pending Home Sales Slip in June; Housing Recovery Remains Choppy Despite Retreat in Interest Rates
By Josh Mitchell
559 words
28 July 2014
06:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—The number of contracts signed to buy previously-owned homes slipped in June, a sign the housing recovery remains choppy despite a retreat in interest rates.

An index of pending home sales, reflecting purchases under contract but not yet closed, fell 1.1% to a reading of 102.7 in June from May, ending three months of gains, the National Association of Realtors said Monday. The above-100 reading indicates market activity was still "average," if not robust, the trade group said.

Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had forecast a 0.5% rise in June sales.

Compared with a year ago, pending-home sales were down 7.3% last month. That suggests many Americans remain unwilling or unable to enter the market despite historically low borrowing costs and a pickup in job creation. Lenders are still imposing strict lending standards, and home prices have risen sharply over the past two years. Meanwhile, Americans' incomes are growing tepidly.

Lawrence Yun, NAR's chief economist, said sales conditions have improved since the winter as price gains have eased and more homes have come on the market. "However, supply shortages still exist in parts of the country, wages are flat, and tight credit conditions are deterring a higher number of potential buyers from fully taking advantage of lower interest rates," Mr. Yun said in a statement.

Pending home sales offers a particularly fresh look at the market because it measures contract signings, which occur weeks or even months before sales become final.

Other recent reports have sent mixed signals on the direction of the housing market.

The NAR's closely watched measure of existing-home sales—which measure sales after contracts close—climbed last month to the highest level since October, the trade group said last week. Those sales, which reflect roughly 90% of the market, have risen for three straight months but remain below the year-ago level.

But sales of newly built homes fell 8.1% in June from the prior month, the Commerce Department said last week.

The housing market had been a catalyst for the broader U.S. economy starting in 2012 but cooled last year after mortgage rates started to rise. Rates this year have fallen, and the market has shown some progress in recent months. But it has yet to regain its earlier momentum, despite the onset of the spring selling season.

Monday's report showed pending sales in June fell 2.9% in the Northeast region, rose 1.1% in the Midwest, fell 2.4% in the South and were mostly flat in the West.

Interest rates could influence the direction of the market. The average rate on a 30-year mortgage has hovered slightly above 4% since the spring and stood at 4.13% this week, according to Freddie Mac. That is down from roughly 4.5% at the start of 2014.

But rates are expected to rise as the economy improves and the Federal Reserve withdraws its support for the economy. The Fed has signaled it will end a bond buying program in October and likely raise short-term interest rates some time in 2015. The Fed has kept rates near zero throughout the recovery to stimulate spending, hiring and investment.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

New-Home Slowdown Pressures Recovery
By Eric Morath and Kris Hudson
1057 words
25 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Demand for new homes slowed sharply during the first half, a development that threatens to reverberate beyond the housing market and throughout the broader economy.

Sales of new single-family homes fell 4.9% through the first six months of the year compared with the same period of 2013, according to Commerce Department data released Thursday. June sales fell 8.1% from the prior month, and the May number was revised down from the best figure in six years to not even the best figure in six months.

John Burns, chief executive of a housing-research firm that bears his name, posits that shell shock from the real-estate bust, mounting student-loan debt and last year's steep increase in new-home prices continues to deter buyers. "I just think there's a real lack of confidence to buy a home and take on a mortgage right now, which is really hurting the housing market," he said.

The lack of vigor in the new-home sector could have wide ramifications for the economy heading into the year's second half, weighing on economic growth and job creation in the well-paying construction sector. Home construction was a major employment and growth engine before the recession -- historically accounting for 5% of the U.S. economy, according to the National Association of Home Builders. That figure has fallen to about 3% in recent years.

After slumping from 2006 to mid-2009, spending on home building and improvements added to gross domestic product during much of the recovery. That trend has reversed. A first-quarter pullback in residential investment contributed to the overall economy contracting at a 2.9% pace. The initial estimate of second-quarter growth is due out next week.

"Housing has clearly been a notable area of persistent sluggishness beyond early-year weather disruptions," said Ted Wieseman, an economist at Morgan Stanley.

The main impediments to home construction have shifted in the past year to weakness in demand rather than supply. Since 2011, builders' major concerns were a lack of lots ready for construction and labor shortages. As demand increased, they focused on building a limited number of homes but selling them at higher prices, which allowed them to boost profit margins.

Now, several factors have combined to hamper demand. Rising property values brought more existing homes to the market, creating cheaper competition for new homes. Sales of previously owned homes rose for the third straight month in June, the National Association of Realtors said earlier this week.

Steep price increases in the past two years put new homes out of reach for some buyers. The $273,500 median price of a new home in June is 17.6% higher than two years earlier and 22% more than the June median price for an existing home.

And while job growth has picked up, wage gains remain tepid and mortgage-qualification standards too strict for many would-be buyers.

The absence of momentum in new-home sales heading into the third quarter, along with no improvement in mortgage applications and still-tight lending standards "raise serious questions about the state of the housing market going forward," said BNP Paribas economist Yelena Shulyatyeva.

Sonal Basu, a Redfin Corp. real-estate agent covering Alameda and Contra Costa counties in the San Francisco Bay Area, said new-home sales have slowed in her market. "Buyers are priced out of the [new-home] market, and there is more inventory on the resale side for them to choose from, as well," she said.

Weaker demand for new homes could curtail construction-employment gains. A slowdown in hiring there would be a concern because those jobs tend to pay more than positions in retail and food services. In the second quarter, the economy added 94,000 retail jobs and 51,000 construction positions.

The National Association of Home Builders calculates that, for every single family home constructed, three full-time jobs are created for a year. Half of those jobs are in construction and the other half in related industries such as manufacturing of construction goods.

Jobs in residential building have increased 2.6% so far this year, outpacing a 1% gain in overall employment. But with signs that building is slowing, the pace of hiring could be difficult to maintain.

The pace of overall hiring has strengthened this year, with employers adding better than 200,000 jobs for five consecutive months, but the gains have yet to translate into better pay for many Americans. Wage growth has only kept pace with inflation during much of the recovery.

"The labor market may be getting better but wage gains are not and until that happens, families will not be buying new homes at any great pace," said Joel Naroff, president of consulting firm Naroff Economic Advisors Inc.

Federal Reserve policy makers have expressed frustration about the housing sector's lackluster recovery. "While this sector has recovered notably from its earlier trough, housing activity leveled off in the wake of last year's increase in mortgage rates, and readings this year have, overall, continued to be disappointing," Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen said earlier this month.

One bright spot: While PulteGroup Inc. on Thursday reported a 2% decline in sales contracts for its second quarter from a year earlier, CEO Richard Dugas Jr. noted the builder sees early signs of a revival among entry-level buyers. Those buyers have been largely sidelined in recent years due to stringent mortgage-qualification standards and tepid job and wage growth.

"We remain optimistic about overall conditions and very encouraged about demand in the first half of the year," he said on a call with investors on Thursday.

Still, Toronto-based Mattamy Homes, which sold 1,400 homes last year in the U.S., said sales in 2014 have so far failed to meet expectations. Mattamy's U.S. sales in June were down more than 10% from a year earlier.

"The U.S. housing market is being held back," Mattamy's Chief Operating Officer Brian Johnston said. Purchasing a new home is "not as desirable as it used to be, given that so many people got burned by buying a new house" when their investment was decimated in the downturn.

Hamptons for the Masses --- Sales Surge of Homes Under $1 Million in Playground of Rich; North Fork Active
By Josh Barbanel
845 words
25 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The Hamptons real-estate market is far more than beach-front mansions for billionaires.

An uptick in deals for lower-priced homes along the storied South Fork of Long Island -- mostly by bargain hunting second-home buyers -- has pushed up the number of sales there to their highest point since 2005.

Even on the island's North Fork, a quieter and more-affordable alternative that is a short drive from the Hamptons, buyers have stepped up activity, and prices have been rising.

The second quarter is the peak selling season on the east end of Long Island, and new market reports celebrate homes priced below $1 million that are moving quickly.

"You don't have to be a millionaire to buy a second home on the East End," said Dottie Herman, president of Douglas Elliman, a real-estate firm.

While prices on the most expensive headline-generating homes have risen to record levels, brokers say, lower-priced houses are still more affordable than they were during the last real-estate boom.

A report by Douglas Elliman released on Thursday noted 700 sales during the second quarter -- 3.7% above the same quarter in 2013. It is the most sales since the second quarter of 2005, when 786 homes traded.

Jonathan Miller, an appraiser and president of Miller Samuel Inc., attributed a rise in sales to "an expansion of entry-level homes, defining entry level as prices below $1 million."

During the second quarter, Mr. Miller said, the number of below-$1 million sales rose 6.6% compared with the year-earlier quarter and were the highest since he began tracking such sales in 2011.

Roger Blaugh, a Corcoran Group broker who is handling the sale of a contemporary house on a canal in East Quogue listed for $999,500, said many properties were priced below $1 million, and some for far less.

He recently helped a buyer, a year-round resident, snag a 1960s ranch house on two-thirds of an acre near Southampton Village. The house was listed at $539,000. "Affordability plays a big factor in the market overall," he said, "even though all the talk seems to be about the mansions and the $30 million price tag."

The median price in the Hamptons, $908,500, remained 17% below the peak prices during the boom, according to the Elliman report. It was also just below the $910,000 median price for a Manhattan apartment during the same quarter.

As sales of lower-priced home rose, the median Hamptons sale price was down 1.3%.

On the North Fork, the median home price of a home was just over half that of a Hamptons home -- $477,500 -- and remained 21% below the 2008 peak. Nevertheless, it up 3.9% from the same quarter a year earlier.

Ernest Cervi, a Corcoran managing director who is based in Bridgehampton, said that Corcoran data showed a significant increase in sales in the North Fork in the second quarter, as new buyers, first-time or second-time home buyers move into the market.

He said interest rates remained attractive, economic indicators were positive and some buyers worried prices might begin to rise.

Traditionally, the North Fork and Hamptons markets have been separate. In part, that reflected the areas' contrasts: the ocean-splashed Hamptons, summertime playground to celebrities and old-money families, versus the North Fork, a more sedate and modest string of communities, many with vineyards, bounded by Long Island Sound and Peconic Bay.

But as prices in the Hamptons rose sharply during the boom, many buyers were priced out. Meanwhile, the North Fork became more appealing with added amenities, from restaurants to high-end retailing.

To be sure, many lower-priced homes need renovation and not all neighborhoods have been seeing a sales surge. Thomas Uhlinger, a Douglas Elliman broker based in Mattituck, has a three-bedroom home in Cutchogue on the North Fork, with a dock on a creek, listed for $895,000 that has been on the market for many months.

He said buyers were looking "but not taking a swing," since inventory was plentiful and rising. But he said many buyers were realizing the North Fork was undervalued.

In recent years, he said most summer buyers came from Manhattan, but now there was a new trend, as younger, successful buyers in their 30s are looking over the East End: "A lot from Brooklyn are coming in," he said.

U.S. News: New-Home Sales Slip, Stir Concerns
By Eric Morath and Kris Hudson
786 words
25 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
7
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The market for newly built homes weakened in the first half of the year, a development that is likely to impede economic growth and could hold back job creation in the well-paying construction sector in the months ahead.

Sales of new single-family homes fell 4.9% through the first six months of the year, compared with the same period of 2013, according to Commerce Department data released Thursday.

June sales fell 8.1% from May to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 406,000. Meanwhile, May sales were revised down to a rate of 442,000 from a previously estimated 504,000. The initial May reading would have been the strongest pace since the recovery began, but the revised figure isn't even the best of the year.

The absence of momentum in new-home sales heading into the third quarter, along with no improvement in mortgage applications and still-tight lending standards, "raise serious questions about the state of the housing market going forward," said BNP Paribas economist Yelena Shulyatyeva.

The latest numbers show home building -- a major employment and growth engine prior the recession -- is still struggling to find its footing.

Residential investment, including home building and improvements, had added to gross-domestic-product gains for 12 straight quarters before declining the last two. The first-quarter pullback contributed to the overall economy contracting at a 2.9% pace. The initial estimate of second-quarter GDP is due next week.

Sales of newly built homes account for about 10% of U.S. home-buying activity, and month-to-month changes in the data can be volatile. Over the longer run, sales began to tumble in 2006 after topping 1.2 million the prior year. The market didn't bottom out until early 2011, well after the most recent recession ended. The sales pace then grew modestly for about two years before slipping again in the second half of 2013, when mortgage interest rates began to rise.

The latest data suggest a continuation of a lackluster recovery. From a year earlier, new-home sales were down 11.5% in June.

Builder Mattamy Homes, which sold 1,400 homes last year in five states, said sales in 2014 have so far failed to meet expectations. Mattamy's U.S. sales in June were more than 10% less than a year earlier.

"The U.S. housing market is being held back," Mattamy's Chief Operating Officer Brian Johnston said before Thursday's report. Purchasing a new home is "not as desirable as it used to be, given that so many people got burned by buying a new house" when their investment was decimated in the downturn.

The supply of homes on the market is increasing. There was a 5.8-month supply of new homes on the market at the end of last month, up from a 5.2-month inventory at the end of April. Thursday's report showed new-home sales fell across the country, with the sharpest decline in the Northeast.

Builders reported varying sales results for June, a month in which sales typically decline modestly on an unadjusted basis from the traditionally strong month of May. Builders in Texas and other states with strong job growth reported robust sales. But those spanning multiple states noted a continued lack of confidence among buyers.

Quarterly results varied among national builders on Thursday. But PulteGroup Inc. noted a glimmer of hope in the challenging market: early signs of a revival of first-time buyers.

Pulte noted that sales at its Centex entry-level brand increased by 26% in the quarter from a year earlier on a per-community basis. Pulte executives called the Centex gains a sign of "broadening improvement" for the first-time segment that warrants watching to see if it continues. Entry-level buyers have been largely sidelined in recent years due to stringent mortgage-qualification standards and tepid wage growth.

John Burns, chief executive of housing research and consultancy John Burns Real Estate Consulting Inc. in Irvine, Calif., said his firm's June survey of 228 builder representatives found a 3% seasonally adjusted decline in new sales contracts in June from May, and a 5% decline from June 2013.

Mr. Burns posits that shell shock from the real-estate bust, mounting student-loan debt and last year's steep increase in new-home prices continue to deter buyers. "I just think there's a real lack of confidence to buy a home and take on a mortgage right now, which is really hurting the housing market," he said.

PulteGroup Inc. said its second-quarter earnings improved as the home builder reported slightly higher revenue boosted by higher average selling prices.

However, Pulte's new orders fell 2.2% from a year earlier to 4,778 homes, though the value of net new orders for the period increased 5% to $1.6 billion. Closings, meanwhile, fell 9%.

Builders have also benefited from a low supply of existing-home inventory in recent periods, although recent data was weaker than expected. Housing starts sank a seasonally adjusted 9.3% in June amid wet weather in the South, while residential housing permits, an indicator of future construction plans, were down 4.2%, according to the Commerce Department. Analysts, however, had indicated that June's results were likely a temporary departure from a trend of recovery in the housing market.

"Our view of the U.S. housing market remains positive, as improvements in both the economy and employment provide ongoing support to an industry already benefiting from low inventory, low mortgage rates, better pricing and favorable demographic trends, said Richard J. Dugas Jr., PulteGroup chairman and chief executive.

Overall, Pulte reported a profit of $41.9 million, or 11 cents a share, up from $36.4 million, or nine cents a share, a year earlier. The latest quarter's results included income tax expense of $26 million—compared with prior-year income tax expense of $2 million—as well as $88 million charges tied to insurance reserves and office relocation costs. The year-ago quarter included $67 million of charges resulting from a contractual dispute, debt repurchases and corporate relocation.

Total revenue edged up 0.5% to $1.29 billion.

Analysts polled by Thomson Reuters had predicted earnings of 25 cents a share on revenue of $1.33 billion.

Home-building cost of revenue fell 5.1%. Home-sale gross margin rose 480 basis points to 23.6%. The average selling price rose 12% to $328,000.

D.R. Horton Inc. said its fiscal third-quarter profit fell despite strong home-building revenue and an increase in new sales orders, as the home builder recorded impairment charges tied to assets in the Chicago area.

The Chicago housing market remains weak, with sales absorptions and returns in these communities performing below management's expectations, the company said. During the quarter, D.R. Horton took actions to increase sales pace, reduce inventories and improve cash flows in those communities, which results in a $54.7 million impairment charge during the quarter, the company added.

D.R. Horton, one of the nation's largest home-building companies, has long been a favorite with industry analysts because its management team avoids risk and it constructs low-price homes accessible to many buyers.

In the latest quarter, D.R. Horton reported earnings of $113.1 million, or 32 cents a share, down from $146 million, or 42 cents a share, a year earlier. The latest quarter's results included the inventory impairment charges, primarily related to active communities in the Midwest region in Chicago that were purchased from 2004 to 2007 and had been previously impaired.

Home-building revenue climbed 28% to $2.10 billion. Net sales orders increased 32% in value to $2.4 billion and 25% in homes to 8,551.

Analysts polled by Thomson Reuters had expected earnings of 49 cents a share on revenue of $2.09 billion.

For the quarter, D.R. Horton said home closings rose 28% in value to $2.1 billion, while the number of homes closed climbed 19% to 7,676.

The company also said its board has increased its quarterly cash dividend by 67% to 6.25 cents a share.

U.S. News: Home Sales Post Third Monthly Gain
By Josh Mitchell
563 words
23 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A broad measure of U.S. home sales climbed for the third consecutive month in June, hitting the highest level since October as the housing market gained steam after a monthslong slump.

Sales of previously owned homes rose 2.6% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 5.04 million last month, the National Association of Realtors said Tuesday. Revisions showed May sales reached a 4.91 million rate, up from an initially reported 4.89 million pace.

The latest rise suggests the housing market rebounded from a harsh winter and that slowing price growth, coupled with expanding inventory, could set the market up for future gains. "We are seeing a more broad-based recovery," said Lawrence Yun, the Realtor trade group's chief economist.

Tuesday's report showed home prices are growing more slowly than in prior months as more homes come on the market. The median sale price for a home last month was $223,300, up 4.3% from a year earlier.

The inventory of homes available for sale climbed 6.5% from a year earlier. At the current pace, it would take 5.5 months to exhaust the supply, close to what economists consider a balanced market.

One reason sales haven't reached year-ago levels is that fewer investors are entering the market, Mr. Yun said. The share of June sales going to investors was flat at about 16%, down from 19% just two years ago. As a result, purchases of "distressed" properties -- such as those in foreclosure -- slipped as a share of overall sales.

First-time buyers are growing slightly, but at 28% of all buyers, they remain a historically small part of the market.

Despite the latest gains, the market hasn't regained its earlier momentum. Compared with a year earlier, sales were down 2.3% last month. At the current pace, sales are roughly at the level they were in 2000, even though the U.S. population has grown, the economy has more jobs and interest rates remain historically low.

Existing-home sales make up roughly 90% of all home purchases. Home sales were robust in 2012 and the first half of 2013, underpinning the U.S. recovery. But sales slowed in the middle of last year as a rise in mortgage rates and a run-up in prices likely scared away some prospective buyers. Harsh winter weather also likely sapped activity.

Borrowing rates have fallen this year, likely helping to boost sales in the crucial spring selling season. The average rate on a 30-year mortgage fell to 4.13% last week, below year-ago levels, according to Freddie Mac.

Still, clouds loom. The Federal Reserve has signaled it will end its bond-buying stimulus program in October; those bond purchases are credited with pushing down interest rates. A rise in interest rates, as anticipated, would raise the cost of purchasing a home at a time when Americans' incomes are growing meekly.

"Without a better pace of income growth, any increase in mortgage rates. . .will take a bite out of home sales," said Richard Moody, chief economist at Regions Financial Corp. "So, while June's report is encouraging, the real test will be whether or not improving sales will be sustained over coming months."

U.S. News: Blame It on the Rain: Housing Starts Slide
By Alan Zibel and Kris Hudson
712 words
18 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- U.S. home construction tumbled in June due to a stretch of wet weather in the South, a decline that analysts said was likely a temporary departure from a trend of recovery in the housing market.

Housing starts sank 9.3% last month to a seasonally adjusted annual pace of 893,000. It was the weakest showing since September 2013 and the second-straight monthly drop, the Commerce Department said Thursday.

The June decline was driven by a nearly 30% drop in the South, the largest monthly decrease on record for that part of the country. Other parts of the U.S., however, posted increases. Construction in the Midwest was up 28%, while the Northeast was up 14%.

The problem in the South, home builders say, is that the region's unusually wet winter and spring limited the number of home lots ready for construction. By June, a shortage of such build-ready lots left several builders unable to start constructing enough homes to meet demand.

Some builders also mentioned that municipal permitting processes are taking longer this year, perhaps due to short staffing in city building departments.

"We've had such a wet winter that the development cycle has elongated," said Terry Russell, chief executive of Atlanta-based builder FrontDoor Communities, which anticipates selling 175 homes this year in Georgia and South Carolina. "It's at least two or three months longer [to develop a home lot], and that has a significant impact on starts."

Brad Hunter, chief economist at home-building research firm Metrostudy, part of Hanley Wood LLC, said the lot-shortage issue spans several Southern states. Other U.S. regions saw little drop-off in their regular production cycles because they don't typically build much through the winter, but the South does. Thus, the South's slowdown in lot development in the winter and spring is slowing its summer construction starts in relation to past years, he said.

The slowdown in the South -- defined as a huge region stretching from Maryland to Texas to Florida -- could result in a pickup in starts later in the summer, analysts said. Within the South, data from the National Weather Service point to a particularly wet spring in Florida, Mississippi, Louisiana and Alabama, among others. Texas, meanwhile, remains in a drought.

Eric Green, global head of research for TD Securities, called the overall report "exceptionally uneven and on balance is not an indication of a significant turn in housing construction."

The report contained a few silver linings: For the entire second quarter, construction of single-family homes was up 4% from a year ago, compared with a 2% yearly decline for the first three months of the year. Permits for single-family homes were up 2.6% from the prior month and housing starts were up 7.5% from a year earlier.

Still, the pace of construction for May was revised lower, showing a 7.3% decline, compared with an initially reported 6.5% decrease, the Commerce Department said.

And new applications for building permits, a bellwether of future construction, declined 4.2% in June from the prior month to a pace of 963,000, the lowest figure since January.

The decline in construction in June was driven by a 9% drop in single-family homes and a nearly 10% drop in apartments and condominiums with at least two units.

Among builders, perspectives are varied on the state of the new-home market. Drees Co., a closely held builder based in Fort Mitchell, Ky., started construction of 140 homes last month, down 2% from May and down 8% from a year earlier.

Mark Williams, finance chief at Drees, attributed the drop in starts to weak sales, slower permitting by municipalities and tepid consumer confidence.

National builder Lennar Corp. said during its quarterly call with investors last month that a pickup in construction for single-family homes will depend on whether mortgage lenders relax their standards. But rival KB Home said during its quarterly call that it has seen encouraging signs, such as the return of first-time buyers in certain markets with strong job growth.

Though the Commerce report was a disappointment overall, there were a few silver linings: For the entire second quarter, construction of single-family homes was up 4% from a year ago, compared with a 2% yearly decline for the first three months of the year. Permits for single-family homes were up 2.6% from the prior month, and housing starts were up 7.5% from a year earlier.

New applications for building permits overall, a bellwether of future construction, declined 4.2% in June from the prior month to a pace of 963,000, the lowest figure since January.

Both housing starts and permits were weaker in June than expected. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had projected starts would rise to a pace of 1.015 million in June and expected permits would increase to a pace of 1.040 million.

The decline in construction was driven by a 9% drop in single-family homes and a nearly 10% drop in apartments and condominiums homes with at least two units.

US
House Passes $11 Billion Highway Repairs Bill; Short-Term Fix Postpones Dealing With a Trust Fund Running Out of Money
By Siobhan Hughes
851 words
15 July 2014
13:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—The House on Tuesday passed a $10.8 billion measure to pay the federal government's share of road and bridge repairs for the next 10 months, a step that would delay until next year the tougher question of how to shore up the trust fund as spending outpaces revenues.

The House voted 367-55 to pass the measure as time was running short to replenish the Highway Trust Fund, which covers about a quarter of the nation's spending on highway and mass-transit programs and will be insolvent next month unless Congress acts. The Transportation Department projects it will have to slow reimbursements to the states, delaying projects and eliminating jobs during the summer construction season, if Congress fails to deliver more money.

The Senate is expected to take up the highway legislation before the August recess.

The White House supports the package, but earlier on Tuesday President Barack Obama chastised lawmakers for coming up with a small-scale patch, saying Congress "shouldn't pat itself on the back for averting disaster for a few months, kicking the can down the road for a few months, careening from crisis to crisis when it comes to something as basic as our infrastructure."

His comments, at the Turner-Fairbank Highway Research Center in McLean, Va., a federal facility investigating technology for safer, more efficient roads, were part of a broader strategy to put pressure on Congress to focus their energy on infrastructure.

Lawmakers on both the left and the right complained that the short-term fix relied on budget gimmicks. The bulk of the money for the 10-month patch would come from a tactic known as pension smoothing, which allows companies to temporarily reduce pension contributions. The tactic raises revenue for the government because companies lose out on tax deductions associated with pension contributions.

The other budget maneuvers include transferring $1 billion from a fund used to cover cleanup costs from leaking underground storage tanks, and a one-year extension, until 2024, of fees for processing passengers and vehicles through customs.

"Congress can do better—and America needs better," said Rep. Peter Welch (D., Vt.), who went further than other Democrats in expressing his dissatisfaction by voting against the bill. "This gimmick of pension smoothing is about creating a pothole in the pension system in order to fix a pothole on the highway."

He said his vote was "a protest" against "this habit that's getting worse" of coming up with short-term fixes by using methods unrelated to highway funding.

Rep. John Fleming (R., La.), who said he reluctantly voted for the bill, also complained that it "borrows money from the future to pay bills today."

Despite the criticism, though, the vote for the measure was overwhelming, reflecting a desire by members in both parties to avoid deep cuts in transportation spending during the summer construction season.

Much of the frustration expressed before Tuesday's vote was over the fact that the bill was temporary. But putting together a long-term highway bill will be a much bigger and more divisive fight over how to fund the programs in the long term.

Some lawmakers, both Democrats and Republicans, support increasing the 18.4 cents-per-gallon gasoline tax, which Congress hasn't increased since 1993, while others are weighing whether to switch to a different system altogether that would charge motorists based on the distance they drive.

More conservative members balk at raising taxes and say that Congress simply needs to do a better job prioritizing spending. Another idea that has picked up bipartisan support is a plan from Rep. John Delaney (D., Md.) to set up a fund financed by businesses in exchange for repatriating money from overseas on a tax-free basis.

Either way, Congress will have to address the problem. Last month, the Congressional Budget Office projected that if gasoline and diesel tax rates remain the same, spending would exceed revenue by $167 billion over the decade through 2024.

House Ways and Means Committee Chairman Dave Camp (R., Mich.) said that he would have preferred a long-term package but, with no deal in sight, a short-term measure lasting through May would give Congress enough time to construct a new plan without jeopardizing transportation projects during the current construction season.

"This won't be an easy task so it's important that Congress has time to have a deliberative, open process rather than trying to hit Americans who are already paying more for gas with a gas-tax hike," Mr. Camp said.

"This Congress has refused to address its responsibilities," Rep. Earl Blumenauer (D., Ore.) said, complaining that the hopes of a solution would be no better in 10 months. "We will be no better off next May to resolve this question. In fact we will be worse off because we'll be in the middle of a presidential campaign, with a new Congress."

Jeffrey Sparshott contributed to this report.

MANSION --- The Sounds of Silence, for a Price --- Home buyers often find that the qualities they look for in a home -- central location, giant windows, an open floor plan -- can translate into disruptive noise after they move in; How developers and home owners are bringing down the volume
By Candace Taylor
1239 words
11 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The problems started the first night Bob Morris and Ira Silverberg moved into their West Village co-op apartment in Manhattan.

"Almost immediately, there was a thundering sound above us," Mr. Morris said. The footfalls of the family upstairs were so heavy they could feel the vibrations, he said. And while he was complaining about the late-night noise, his downstairs neighbor complained about him, saying she could hear him snoring. Sometimes, she banged on her ceiling with a broomstick.

When such problems arise, "your heart sinks," Mr. Morris said. "You just bought this place that you think is so beautiful. You don't listen long enough while you're looking to understand what the sound situation is."

After months of back-and-forth talks with the neighbors, the couple hired Manhattan-based City Soundproofing to replace their ceilings. The cost? About $30,000.

In the past, many urbanites accepted noisy neighbors, and neighborhoods, as part of life in the city. But homeowners are becoming more demanding about their homes and less tolerant of intrusions. "People spend a lot of money on an apartment; when they get home, they don't want to hear their neighbors' techno music," said Benjamin Sachwald, vice president and director of acoustics, noise and vibration at AKRF, a New York consulting, engineering and planning firm.

As formerly industrial areas are reborn as residential neighborhoods, the characteristics many home buyers prize -- floor-to-ceiling windows, open layouts and proximity to public transportation -- can make them more vulnerable to noise. And in today's high-tech homes, mechanical noises -- from heating and cooling systems, elevators, spa filters and pumps, wine-cellar compressors and media-room units -- also cause unwanted noise.

Developers are pouring money into new buildings to combat the problem. At Oceana Bal Harbour in Florida, for example, developers are pitching the condos as having an acoustical rating above local requirements for noise limits. "Soundproofing is one of the characteristics that high-end buyers look for," said Marcos Corti Maderna, CEO at Consultatio Real Estate, North America.

The Argentina-based developer is spending more than $2 million to envelop the exterior of the building in three layers of glass -- two laminated layers separated from a layer of impact glass by an inch-thick pocket of air -- rather than the single layer typical in Miami-area residential buildings. All the flooring will consist of a 10-inch concrete slab topped by a rubber mat, and insulation will be added between floors and ceilings. Pipes will have springs, and rubber hangers and gaskets to still vibrations.

Perhaps most significantly, Mr. Maderna added, the company will set the building about 350 feet back from Collins Avenue, rather than the required 100 feet. That means "sacrificing square footage," he said, but is worth it to appeal to luxury buyers.

At the Charles condo building in Manhattan, developer Bluerock Real Estate also installed triple-paned windows -- in the apartments facing busy First Avenue -- and took care to cut noise from heating and cooling systems, said Phil Mendlow, a senior vice president at Bluerock. "It's important for developers to carefully abate the noise generated by the plethora of mechanical equipment in these buildings," he said. "If it's not done right, it can be pretty annoying."

Soundproofing solutions can range from the simple -- installing carpeting to help muffle sound -- to the high-tech -- putting in so-called floating floors, which aren't rigidly connected to the building structure.

Mr. Morris's building, built in the early 1970s, has a wooden joist system that lets sounds travel between apartments, said Mason Wyatt, founder of City Soundproofing. When adding carpeting and vinyl padding didn't eliminate the noise, the couple called City Soundproofing. The company replaced the ceilings with two layers of drywall surrounded by a paint-like substance called Green Glue, a viscoelastic polymer. Such damping compounds reduce vibrations and, thus, noise, Mr. Wyatt said. He also sealed the ceiling with acoustical caulk and put in "isolation clips," or fasteners that act as shock absorbers, to further curb vibrations.

The best solution to noise problems is to avoid them in the first place. San Francisco-based architect and soundproofing expert W. David Seidel said that before buying a home, potential residents should visit at different times of day, "when the neighbors' units come to life with kids and parents returning from school or work." Even better is to ask to make a 24-hour recording of the space that will pick up anything unusual, "such as violin practice that starts at 10 p.m. or a flight pattern that changes in the early morning."

Designer Bob Novogratz and his wife, Cortney, bought their home in Manhattan's West Village five years ago for about $10 million, demolishing the two-story building that was on the property and building a five-story, five-bedroom, 7,200-square-foot townhouse with an elevator and roof deck. To take advantage of Hudson River views, the couple installed custom-made, floor-to-ceiling windows on nearly every floor. They soon realized those windows made audible the constant hum of traffic from nearby West Side Highway.

That is a common problem in homes with large windows, Mr. Novogratz said. "The rule is, the bigger the window, the more noise," he said.

So they added a third pane of glass to windows on the west-facing wall, to the tune of $100,000. The hefty price tag was worth it, Mr. Novogratz said, especially where the resale value is concerned. The home is now on the market with Town Residential and Douglas Elliman for $21.5 million. "If someone's going to pay $20 million, it has to be perfect," he said.

Melanie Dunea and Nigel Parry's loft is on a cobblestone street in Tribeca, in a building constructed in the late 1800s as a warehouse for paper storage. When they first saw the condo, the couple loved it for the bright, open layout and the 7-foot windows overlooking a canopy of trees around the Holland Tunnel exit.

"I remember thinking how glorious it was to see trees," said Ms. Dunea. But as they discovered after moving in, they had underestimated the traffic noise. Worse, they also found sounds traveled easily into their bedroom from the apartment above.

"We could hear every single thing upstairs was doing," Mr. Parry said. For one, they were awakened nearly every day by the neighbor's alarm clock -- especially annoying because both Ms. Dunea and Mr. Parry are freelance photographers who work irregular hours.

After several months, they hired a soundproofing company. Based on the company's recommendation, the couple dropped the ceiling in their bedroom about a foot -- covering the original beams -- to allow the installation of foam and other soundproofing materials. They also covered the exposed brick behind their bed with Sheetrock. Finally, the couple hired a company called CitiQuiet to install a third layer of glass on the bedroom windows. They estimated total costs at about $30,000.

"After that, it was very peaceful," Ms. Dunea said.

WASHINGTON—Congress struggled to engineer a plan to inject more money into the fund that covers the federal share of road, bridge and mass-transit spending amid a dispute over whether a temporary fix should extend into next year after midterm elections.

Senate Finance Committee Chairman Ron Wyden (D., Ore.) missed a self-imposed deadline to release a new version of a highway bill, leaving at risk his plans to clear the measure through his committee this week.

Some Republicans on Mr. Wyden's panel, meanwhile, threw their weight behind an alternative devised by House Ways and Means Committee Chairman Dave Camp (R., Mich.) .

Mr. Camp's bill would fund the Highway Trust Fund through May 2015. Mr. Wyden has sought to provide money for the fund through the end of 2014, to buy time for Congress to this year pass a multiyear highway bill that would put federal highway spending on more solid financial footing.

House Speaker John Boehner (R., Ohio) said the House was likely to vote on Mr. Camp's legislation next week.

Speaking to reporters, Mr. Camp argued for a highway-spending bill that lasted through most of the spring season, saying the construction industry needs to be able to use the winter to plan for construction projects when the weather improves.

"If you end at the calendar year, that means you're not prepared for the next construction season," Mr. Camp told reporters.

Democrats complained that extending the temporary fix into next year would simply delay agreement on a multiyear bill to fund road and bridge repairs to next year. Next year will be the start of a new Congress determined by the November elections, when Republicans have the potential to regain control of the Senate.

"There's no reason to think that punting on this issue to the next Congress yet again will magically yield a different outcome," said Tom Carper (D., Del.), a member of the Senate Finance Committee. "Kick the can down the road hasn't served us well."

The fight between the two committees masked a series of smaller, intraparty disagreements over the highway bill. Sen. Bob Corker (R., Tenn.) on Wednesday criticized Mr. Camp's bill as "budget gimmickry," complaining that by turning to funding sources such as allowing companies to delay contributions to pensions, lawmakers were engaging in "political cowardice."

European stocks fell Monday after weaker-than-expected German industrial data encouraged investors to take profit on last week's gains.

The Stoxx Europe 600 index shed 0.9% to 344.80, following a 1.7% gain last week.

Germany's DAX fell 1% to 9906.07. Data showed that industrial production in the country declined in May at the sharpest rate since April 2012, weighed down by a fall in both manufacturing and construction output.

That led Barclays' economists to trim their outlook for second-quarter German gross domestic product to a quarter-on-quarter increase of just 0.1% from a rise of 0.4% previously.

"In light of these weak monthly figures, it is likely that industrial production has declined markedly in the second quarter overall," economist Thomas Harjes wrote in a note.

Lee Hardman, a currency economist at Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi UFJ, wrote in a note that this was a further signal that Europe's economic recovery remains "relatively weak and fragile," which is unlikely to prompt investors to reassess the European Central Bank's currently dovish policy stance.

Last week, the ECB council kept its main lending rate at 0.15% and maintained a -0.1% rate on bank funds deposited with the central bank.

Rates will stay at current levels for "an extended period," ECB President Mario Draghi said at a news conference. Even though he didn't specify a time frame, many analysts now expect the period to last at least through 2016, given the ECB's inflation forecasts.

The U.K.'s FTSE 100 fell 0.6% to 6823.51, and France's CAC 40 slid 1.4% to 4405.76.

In the U.S., stocks also pulled back, after hitting record highs last week. The Dow Jones Industrial Average slipped 44.05 points, or 0.3%, to 17024.21. The S&P 500 fell 7.79 points, or 0.4%, to 1977.65. The Nasdaq Composite Index shed 34.40 points, or 0.8%, to 4451.53.

Traders reported subdued volumes as market participants awaited further cues, including the start of the second-quarter earnings season, which unofficially kicks off Tuesday when Alcoa reports results. Many investors say the coming corporate earnings season will provide a test for the stocks given their near-record levels.

"What took this market up from 1500 to 2000 [in the S&P 500] was that investors have decided that maybe we've entered a more sustainable growth rate," said Jim Paulsen, chief investment strategist at the $350 billion fund manager Wells Capital Management. "When I look at the second quarter I want to see some evidence of sustainability."

Earnings among companies in the S&P 500 are expected to rise 4.6% in the second quarter, after posting a 2.2% climb in a first quarter damped by the harsh winter, according to FactSet.

On Wednesday, the Federal Reserve is due to publish minutes of its rate-setting Federal Open Market Committee, which could shed more light on the timetable for potential monetary action.

Crude for August delivery lost 53 cents a barrel to $103.53 on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold for July delivery lost $3.90 a troy ounce, or 0.3%, to $1,316.50 on the Comex division of Nymex.

Dutch postal company PostNL was the largest gainer on the pan-European index Monday, with shares adding 19% after the company raised its full-year guidance following a strong mail performance in the Netherlands.

Archer Daniels Midland on Monday agreed to buy WILD Flavors, a Swiss food-ingredients maker, for 2.2 billion euros ($3 billion). Shares of ADM rose 1.6%.

Life and Style
Weapons of Mass Construction
By Joe Queenan
643 words
3 July 2014
12:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Everyone knows that the health of the American economy depends a lot on housing starts. If the home-building industry is not doing well, the economy is failing. Life in these United States is a zero-sum game. We are all in this thing together.

I hate being all in this thing together. Or let's just say, I hate being all in this thing together with the home-construction industry. Right now, a McMansion the size of the Louvre is going up directly across the street from my house. Nine other monstrosities are also being deployed in what was once a beautiful, empty meadow. The field has been filled with backhoes and earth movers and building materials on and off for at least two years.

The projects, once begun, take forever to finish. The crew starts work on a house, then gets dispatched to finish another project in a different town, and then comes back. So it takes months to get the micro-chateaux built. It's like watching someone set fire to your neighborhood, then douse it, then come back and start the fire again six weeks later. You'd rather they just ruined things once and for all and got it over with. If you're going to sack Rome, sack it. Drilling, digging, dust and leveled trees have been our reality since 2011. It makes it very, very hard to root for the home builders.

I am constantly reading that young people are not buying houses at the pace needed to get the economy percolating. Well, maybe someone should tell the developers to stop building lurid, vile houses that no one can afford. Or to stop building lurid, vile, prefab, ticky-tacky houses even if people can afford them.

When the economy cratered in 2008 and my 401(k) got massacred, I wasn't as upset as I should have been because it meant that the McMansions scheduled to be erected across the street wouldn't get built until the recession was over. Four happy years ensued, without bogus cathedral windows and four-car garages and faux-Belgian cobblestones and Philistines for neighbors. This situation put me in the uncomfortable position of having to root against my own country. As long as the housing industry was flat on its back, life was good.

I really wish that the economy were not so dependent upon the health of home builders. I would love to root for these guys. I really would. But they build trash. They tear down adorable bungalows and build McMansions in Princeton, N.J. In Chicago, in Boston, in Los Angeles and even in little old Easton, Pa., they are bulldozing whatever stands in their way and throwing up their eyesores. Throwing up being the operative term.

Unlike the car guys, who build products that give me pleasure, or the pizzeria makers, who bake tasty pizza, or the clothing industry, which makes snappy shirts, fantastic trousers, fabulous anoraks and some pretty nifty chapeaus, the housing industry builds malignant, socially corrosive, architecturally putrescent stuff that I hate. The house-building folks fall into the same category as the leaf-blower, the weed-whacker and post-office guys. Ugh.

I dream of an America whose national well-being would depend upon the health of the latte industry, the antivirus industry, perhaps even the flavored-cigar industry.

"The Dow rose 170 points today, buoyed by positive news from the apps industry" is news I want to hear. "Craft vermouth startups grew at a 5.6% pace in August" would also be terrific. Ditto: "The squid ink pasta industry is coming back with a roar!"

But frankly, I don't expect this to happen. Americans are a band of brothers. In my neck of the woods, a band of Toll Brothers.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

World
High Property Prices Drive New Zealand Immigration Debate; Smaller Parties Seek to Capitalize on Migrant Issue Ahead of Sept. 20 Election
By Lucy Craymer
1002 words
2 July 2014
22:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WELLINGTON, New Zealand—As New Zealand gears up for a hard-fought election, an issue that typically confronts troubled economies has crept onto the agenda of this largely prosperous nation—immigration.

As Sept. 20 approaches, parties across the political spectrum are busy crafting anti-immigration policies that they hope will lure voters. Among the proposals are a clampdown on foreign visas and a plan to restrict property investment by forcing newcomers to live in small regional towns.

A sudden upsurge in nationalistic fervor is usually associated with economies that are in the doldrums, where immigrants are targeted by groups or individuals with frustrated ambitions. The euro zone's economic woes have allowed more nationalistic, or "euroskeptic" parties, to gain ground in recent elections, for example.

New Zealand's economy, though, is anything but troubled. The South Pacific nation, with a population of 4.5 million, is outperforming most major economies thanks to booming demand in Asia for the country's meat and dairy exports. The economy has outpaced the U.K. and U.S. in recent years; it grew by a healthy 3.3% in the year through March.

The central bank has become so convinced of the economy's health that it has started to raise interest rates. Few other developed economies have dared to raise rates since the financial crisis. New Zealand's benchmark cash-rate target has risen three times this year to 3.25%.

That stellar economic performance has lured more foreigners to New Zealand. In addition, it has prompted citizens living overseas to return home and encouraged more New Zealanders to stay put in the country. As a result, the small island nation—a net exporter of migrants as little as two years ago—is experiencing its strongest immigration flows in more than a decade.

Government figures show that 3,980 more foreign nationals and New Zealanders took up residence in the country in May than emigrated—2,310 more than a year earlier. In addition, 4,690 more foreign nationals took up residence than left, 1,150 more than a year earlier.

Under normal circumstances, immigration would be a boon to the economy by increasing demand for local goods and services. However, the influx of new arrivals has triggered an unwelcome side effect: skyrocketing house prices, which are pushing accommodation costs beyond the reach of many ordinary New Zealanders.

According to the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, New Zealand has the world's fourth-most expensive housing market on a price-to-income and rent-ratio basis—pricier than the U.K., U.S. and Australia, which also has a booming property market.

In the past year, house prices have risen 10% in Auckland, New Zealand's largest city, where the average price of a property, from studio apartments to waterfront mansions, is now 611,000 New Zealand dollars (US$535,458).

For house hunters like Natalie Sampson, a single 33-year-old teacher at an Auckland day-care center, the high prices make promises to rein in the housing market politically appealing. "It's really important they do something because there are so many people in my situation that just can't afford to buy a house," said Ms. Sampson, who has been searching unsuccessfully for an affordable property for six months.

Immigration is far from being the dominant worry among citizens ahead of an election dominated by concern over how to maintain the strong pace of economic growth and talk of speeding up the rebuilding of Christchurch, New Zealand's second-biggest city, which was devastated by earthquakes in 2011.

Still, under the country's complex proportional-representation system, where smaller parties wield considerable influence, otherwise marginal issues can assume greater importance. The New Zealand First party, which takes one of the toughest lines on immigration among its peers, could well emerge as a kingmaker in the September election.

The party has called for some immigrants to be sent to live in regional towns for the first five years of their stay, so they can contribute to local economies—rather than being allowed to settle in larger cities like Auckland and Christchurch, where property prices are more of a concern.

New Zealand First also wants to restrict foreigners living outside New Zealand from investing in local property; the party blames the government for being too lenient in recent years.

The ruling National party, which has an interest in playing down the immigration issue, has been in power for six years and remains popular. But its three coalition partners, the Maori Party, United First, and Act, have seen their popularity wane recently.

Recent opinion polls show the National party's support at just short of a majority, while backing for the main opposition parties, Labour and the Greens, is close to 40% together.

The Labour party has said it would look at trying to halve the number of incoming migrants, as well as allowing foreign investors only to purchase new properties to help prevent a housing crisis from worsening. The center-left party's likeliest coalition partner, the Greens, support similar restrictions on offshore property investment.

New Zealand's central bank has tried to skim the froth off the housing market recently by raising interest rates and regulating mortgage lending. However, such measures have often made it more difficult for first-time home buyers to get onto the first rung of the housing ladder.

It is difficult to know for sure how many nonresidents, including overseas investors, own property in New Zealand. Economists' estimate the figure could be anywhere from 3% to 20% across the country.

"Anecdotally, you would say they are the marginal buyer, but in terms of proving that, where the government falls down is in not actually having information to draw a policy conclusion from," said Chris Green, head of research at brokerage First NZ Capital.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.S. News: Home Sales Show Signs Of Gaining New Steam
By Ben Leubsdorf
541 words
1 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A gauge of pending home sales jumped in May, the latest evidence that the U.S. housing recovery began to regain its footing this spring even if it isn't firing on all cylinders.

The National Association of Realtors' index tracking pending sales of existing homes rose a seasonally adjusted 6.1% in May from the prior month to 103.9, the group said Monday.

The increase was the index's largest monthly gain since April 2010, when home sales spiked 9.6% ahead of the expiration of a tax credit for first-time buyers of homes.

"It looks like May was a good month for home sales. . .and many of the housing indicators we track have pointed to a pickup in the housing market over the past few months following its recent earlier slump," J.P. Morgan Chase economist Daniel Silver said in a note to clients.

Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had expected pending home sales to rise a more modest 1.1% in May. April's reading was raised slightly to 97.9 from the initial estimate of 97.8.

The pending-sales index can be a timely measure of sales activity because it tracks home purchases when contracts are signed. Sales usually close a month or two later.

Pending sales have risen month-over-month for three straight months. But they still were down 5.2% in May from a year earlier.

"It is important to bear in mind that with mortgage applications for home purchase not showing any signs of sustained strength. . .it is apparent that much of the juice in the existing-home sales market remains centered in all-cash purchases by speculative buyers," MFR Inc. economist Joshua Shapiro said in a note to clients.

Still, the sign of strength in pending sales accompanies improvements in other gauges of U.S. housing-market activity. Those indicators fell with last summer's jump in borrowing costs and slid through a harsh winter.

Sales of new homes soared a seasonally adjusted 18.6% in May to their highest level in six years, the Commerce Department said.

Sales of existing homes rose a seasonally adjusted 4.9%, according to the National Association of Realtors.

The Federal Reserve, however, has flagged sluggishness in the housing market as a concern for the overall economy. Longer-term readings on the housing market show new-home sales were up just 0.8% in the first five months of 2014 compared with a year earlier, and existing-home sales were down 5% in May from a year earlier.

Lawrence Yun, the National Association of Realtors' chief economist, said home sales are improving but the weak first quarter took a heavy toll. He expects 2.8% fewer sales of existing homes in 2014 compared with 2013.

"Sales should exceed an annual pace of five million homes in some of the upcoming months behind favorable mortgage rates, more inventory and improved job creation," Mr. Yun said.

"However, second-half sales growth won't be enough to compensate for the sluggish first quarter and will likely fall below last year's total."

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

A gauge of pending home sales jumped in May, the latest evidence that the U.S. housing recovery began to regain its footing this spring even if it isn't firing on all cylinders.

The National Association of Realtors' index tracking pending sales of existing homes rose a seasonally adjusted 6.1% in May from the prior month to 103.9, the group said Monday. It was the index's largest monthly gain since April 2010, when home sales spiked 9.6% ahead of the expiration of a tax credit for first-time buyers.

"It looks like May was a good month for home sales…and many of the housing indicators we track have pointed to a pickup in the housing market over the past few months following its recent earlier slump," J.P. Morgan Chase economist Daniel Silver said in a note to clients.

Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had expected pending home sales to rise a more modest 1.1% in May. April's reading was raised slightly to 97.9 from the initial estimate of 97.8. The pending-sales index can be a timely measure of sales activity because it tracks home purchases when contracts are signed. Sales usually close a month or two later.

Pending sales have risen month-over-month for three straight months. But they still were down 5.2% in May from a year earlier.

"It is important to bear in mind that with mortgage applications for home purchase not showing any signs of sustained strength…it is apparent that much of the juice in the existing-home sales market remains centered in all-cash purchases by speculative buyers," MFR Inc. economist Joshua Shapiro said in a note to clients.

Still, the sign of strength in pending sales accompanies improvements in other gauges of U.S. housing-market activity, after they fell with last summer's jump in borrowing costs and slid through a harsh winter. Sales of new homes soared a seasonally adjusted 18.6% in May to their highest level in six years, the Commerce Department said. Sales of existing homes rose a seasonally adjusted 4.9%, according to the National Association of Realtors.

The Federal Reserve, however, has flagged sluggishness in the housing market as a concern for the overall economy. Longer-term readings on the housing market show new-home sales were up just 0.8% in the first five months of 2014 compared with a year earlier, and existing-home sales were down 5% in May from a year earlier.

Lawrence Yun, the National Association of Realtors' chief economist, said home sales are improving but the weak first quarter took a heavy toll. He expects 2.8% fewer sales of existing homes in 2014 compared with 2013.

"Sales should exceed an annual pace of five million homes in some of the upcoming months behind favorable mortgage rates, more inventory and improved job creation," Mr. Yun said. "However, second-half sales growth won't be enough to compensate for the sluggish first quarter and will likely fall below last year's total."
