Personal Finance
HealthCare.gov to Allow Automatic Re-enrollments; Move Will Make It Easier for Consumers to Keep Plans and Retain Subsidies
941 words
28 June 2014
20:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The Obama administration plans to allow consumers to automatically renew for next year the health plans and premium subsidies that they got through the Affordable Care Act's federal exchange.

The move, which will apply to most of the five million people who picked insurance plans through HealthCare.gov for 2014, will make it easier for consumers to stay in their plans next year and retain tax credits lowering their cost of coverage. It also will relieve pressure on the federal exchange, which was crippled during parts of its first enrollment period.

The Department of Health and Human Services said it would allow about 95% of people who signed up for coverage to be automatically re-enrolled if they don't return to the site to switch plans. People who don't take proactive steps to come back to the site and report changes in their income would also have their tax credits renewed based on the information the federal government has on file about them.

The move is likely to be welcomed by big insurers who signed up large numbers of people in the first year by offering aggressively low premiums, since they are banking on many of those consumers choosing to stick with their current coverage by default. Smaller insurers who were hoping to compete afresh for consumers, possibly by lowering their rates to undercut the bigger carriers, could be disadvantaged.

Supporters of the health law had feared some of the new enrollees wouldn't go back to the site for 2015, and that enrollment workers would have to scramble to reach out to those people while at the same time making a big push to sign up more uninsured individuals. Another concern was that without automatic renewal people would lose subsidies that offset their premiums significantly.

About 87% of the people who got coverage through HealthCare.gov also got a tax credit that offsets the cost of their premiums.

Louise Radnofsky

A New Look at Degrees

As states for the first time mine certain degree data from public colleges, they are finding salaries for holders of associate degrees in technical fields are outstripping many of those with four-year degrees, at least early in a career.

The growing body of data takes a sober new look at the value of a postsecondary education in a slowly recovering economy. Overall, the findings affirm that getting a college degree is worth it. But they question the long-held faith that a four-year college degree is the surest ticket to the middle class and an associate degree is its poor country cousin.

New York Federal Reserve Bank economists recently found that the annualized return on a bachelor's and associate degree has long been the same over the course of a career, about 15%. The difference in wages between the two also has remained constant: Working-age bachelor's holders with a full-time job on average make $65,800 annually, and associate's holders make $46,300.

Mark Peters

Existing-Home Sales Rise

Sales of existing homes rose 4.9% in May to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.89 million, the National Association of Realtors said. The pace was the highest since October, the last month annualized sales topped five million.

Coupled with an improvement in April, the May figure points to a housing market that has bounced back from a dismal winter and shows buyers are adjusting to interest rates that are up modestly from spring 2013.

Still, May sales were down 5% from the year-earlier level. The recent rebound could slow if mortgage rates rise, as many economists project. Because of the sluggish start to 2014, the Realtors group expects a 3% reduction in sales this year from 2013's total of 5.09 million.

Eric Morath

Big Funds Missed Rally

Corporate pension funds and university endowments in the U.S. have missed out on much of the rally for stocks since 2009, following a push to diversify into other investments that have had disappointing performances.

The institutions, ranging from General Motors to Harvard University, have been shifting to hedge funds, private equity and venture capital, "alternative investments" that outpaced stocks during the 2008 meltdown but have badly lagged behind the S&P 500 since 2009.

The U.S. companies with the largest defined-benefit pension plans in 2013 posted an average return of 9.9%, according to a survey of 100 large firms by Milliman, which provides actuarial products and services. The S&P 500 returned 32% in 2013, including dividends.

Gregory Zuckerman

Go Southwest, Young Man

The U.S. economy seems headed to a long-run growth rate well below 3%, but some urban areas will grow faster than 4% annually for the rest of the decade, according to a study released by the U.S. Conference of Mayors.

Almost all the big-growth areas are in the South and West. Of the 21 highest-growth metro regions, six are in Texas and four, in Florida. Construction, energy, computer jobs and professional business services will be major sources of the growth, the study says. Heading the list is Midland, Texas, with growth projected at 5.8%, helped by agriculture, construction and mining.

The biggest loser will be upstate New York. In the forecast, five of the 10 slowest economies will be in New York state. At the very bottom, Utica-Rome and Binghamton will grow just 1% a year through 2020.

Kathleen Madigan

U.S. News: New-Home Sales Back on Track After Chill
By Kris Hudson and Joe Light
826 words
25 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New-home sales soared in May, but the gains simply put the market back on track to match last year's overall pace, rather than signaled a significant momentum gain.

Home builders, economists and analysts described the May increase as a delayed rebound to normal sales levels after a weak start to the spring selling season, which was hampered by harsh weather and the fitful economic recovery. Many cautioned that one or two more months of strong gains are needed to prove that the housing recovery indeed has gained steam.

The U.S. Commerce Department reported Tuesday that contracts to sell newly built homes in May amounted to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 504,000, up a heady 18.6% from the rate in April, and up 16.9% from the May 2013 pace.

However, sales on a year-to-date basis show that the May gain simply amounted to catch-up for weak sales earlier in 2014. Builders sold roughly 194,000 homes in this year's first five months, nearly matching the 193,000 from the same period of 2013, Commerce data shows.

Economists noted that the monthly Commerce figures can be volatile and prone to later revisions. The April-to-May increase of 18.6% was the steepest one-month jump since January 1992, but it came with a margin of error of plus or minus 17.3 percentage points.

Some economists said the May increase indicates a general rebound in consumer confidence. U.S. job growth has returned to healthy levels, with more than 200,000 jobs added in each of the past four months. Interest rates on 30-year, fixed-rate mortgages remain historically low at roughly 4.17%. And home builders generally are more confident now than earlier in 2014.

However, entry-level buyers remain largely sidelined by stringent mortgage-qualification standards and tepid wage growth, economists said.

"We're going to want to see the June and July numbers to make sure that the [upward] trend is real, that we've exited a soft patch," said Robert Dietz, an economist with the National Association of Home Builders.

Jon Durden, 36 years old, has a contract to buy a new home in Brentwood, Calif., in September for roughly $503,000. Mr. Durden, who went under contract for the home in May, said that he and his family first looked at existing homes but didn't find anything that fit their needs.

Mr. Durden said that sellers of previously owned homes in his area favor buyers who can pay all cash or can make a large down payment, something that his family couldn't afford to do. "Cash is king out there right now," he said. "It's tough."

On the other hand, the preferred lender of TRI Pointe Homes Inc., the new-home builder he is buying from, offered to cover $5,000 in closing costs if he chose it. TRI threw in free upgrades, including a solar electricity system, he said.

His real-estate agent, Jodi Marfia of Sharp Realty, said new construction has been especially popular among first-time home buyers, who can't compete with all-cash offers made on many low-price homes in her area.

"They don't have to fight with 26 other offers" when buying a new home, Ms. Marfia said.

Doug Duncan, chief economist at mortgage-finance giant Fannie Mae, is among those who say the May increase might have partly been a bounce from a poor spring. "The first quarter was a disaster," he said, noting that a recent pickup in employment might also be leading to increased sales.

Mr. Duncan said the May rebound might improve confidence among lenders to extend credit to home builders to buy land and build new homes. That could help "rebuild the whole food chain" that leads to more new homes being built, he said.

Home-building consultant John Burns is among those who consider the May rise an aberration. A survey of 230 home-builder executives by John Burns Real Estate Consulting Inc. found a 3% rise in new-home sales contracts in May from April.

One factor seen as limiting home sales -- rising prices -- wasn't a deterrent in May. The median price of new homes put under contract in May was $282,000, up 4.6% from April and up 6.9% from May 2013.

There was a 4.5-month supply of new homes on the market at the end of May, down from a 5.3-month inventory in April.

May's rise was led by the Northeast, where sales surged 54.5% from April, and the West, where sales were up 34%. They rose 14.2% in the South and ticked up 1.4% in the Midwest.

The Property Report
Apartment Market Rolls On
By Chelsey Dulaney
736 words
25 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Investors in the past week have closed on two separate purchases of apartment complexes in the Denver region and Washington -- for a total of $324 million -- in the latest sign of the surprising endurance of the multifamily sector.

A venture including the Guardian Life Insurance Company of America and Allstate Life Insurance Co. bought a $229 million portfolio of 1,514 units in Denver, Aurora, Colo., and Renton, Wash. Radco Cos., an Atlanta-based real-estate investment firm, acquired a $95 million apartment complex with 512 units in the Lakewood suburb of Denver.

The buyers said projected yields were 6% and 5.9%, respectively. That is a decline from average yields in the Denver area of 6.3% in the 12 months ending on March 30. Falling yields typically represent rising prices.

The continued strength of the multifamily sales market has been a relief to many landlords who were worried the market would weaken. Rental apartment buildings were among the first types of commercial properties to rebound after the recession. But as early as 2011, some analysts were predicting the sector would cool off, fearing competition from improving home prices and the fledgling single-family rental market.

Those thunderclouds passed without a storm. The competition from the sales market has been weaker than expected largely because mortgage lending has continued to be restrictive.

Rental apartment values nationally are up 14% from the peak 2007 levels hit before the downturn, according to a Green Street Advisors index that tracks the performance of listed rental-apartment landlords. Sales volume in Denver increased 15% in the first quarter compared with a year ago, according to Real Capital Analytics Inc., a research firm.

Rents and occupancy rates also are up nationwide. In the first quarter of this year, rents rose another 0.6% -- up 13% since the upswing began in 2009 -- and vacancies fell to 4%, according to real-estate data firm Reis Inc.

Denver has been one of the strongest housing markets in the country, fueled by a high-tech and startup scene that has launched companies including Chipotle Mexican Grill Inc. and Rally Software Development. Denver has seen 2.8% job growth in the year ending in April, compared with 1.7% nationally, according to Green Street Advisors analyst Dave Bragg.

Property prices in Denver are up 23% since 2007, the biggest rise of any U.S. city outside of San Francisco, according to Green Street. Denver's growing job market "bodes very, very well for" its rental market, Mr. Bragg said.

Mike Moran, who leads Allstate's real-estate investment group, says the Denver portfolio is appealing because it consists of "workforce housing" with monthly rents ranging from $1.20 to $1.35 a square foot. Few developers are building such housing right now, he said, leaving the market wide open for high returns.

Dan Fasulo, managing director of Real Capital, also said workforce housing has less risk than upscale apartments because there is less new supply in the pipeline. "If the overall economy does improve, you'll basically reap the benefits," he said.

TruAmerica Multifamily, an investment firm founded last year by industry veteran Bob Hart and Guardian Life Insurance, was part of the buying group and will manage the portfolio.

TruAmerica and its partners will spend between $25 million and $30 million renovating the apartment buildings, which were built in the 1970s and '80s. Mr. Hart said he hopes to see a 20% to 25% rise in rents over the next five years.

Radco plans to spend about $1.2 million on renovations including updating the clubhouse, swimming pool and dog spa. "Denver is a dog town," said Radco founder Norman Radow.

Still, some question whether Denver's housing market can maintain this trajectory. Denver's rents have been on the rise for the past five years, up 20% since peak '07 levels. "You can't do 10% rent increases every year," Mr. Fasulo said.

Others worry that an oversupply of upscale housing will flood the market. The supply of multifamily housing in Denver is expected to rise 8.1% in the next four years, according to Green Street.

But Mr. Radow thinks his firm is getting in on the front end of the trend. "We think the rent curve is continuing to go up," he said.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Heard on the Street
Hold Off on America's House Party
By Justin Lahart
535 words
25 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

Prospects for housing have improved, but it is hardly the time for hosannas.

The National Association of Realtors on Monday reported that sales of existing (or previously owned) homes in May rose 4.9% from April, seasonally adjusted. Better still, on Tuesday the Commerce Department reported that new-home sales gained 18.6% in May, reaching a six-year high. Even after allowing for the large error bars around the new home-sales figures, in particular, it seems like housing is escaping its recent funk.

To some extent, the improvement in sales is probably a payback for a tough winter. But slowing home-price appreciation may have had something to do with it as well.

On Tuesday, the latest reading of the S&P/Case-Shiller 20-City Composite Home Price Index, for April, was up 10.8% from a year before. The March reading had shown a 12.4% annual gain. The Federal Housing Finance Agency's less-closely watched house-price index -- a more timely measure because, unlike the S&P/Case-Shiller, it isn't based on a three-month average -- showed monthly price gains halted in April.

It may be that the supply problems that have pushed prices higher are finally alleviating. That matters because with home prices outpacing incomes, the possibility of owning has been getting further out of reach for many would-be first-time buyers. Meanwhile, owners hoping to move into something bigger or nicer have seen the price difference between their current homes and their dream homes expand to the point that many are opting to just stay put.

Absent more first-time buyers, and more trading activity, home sales will be stuck at levels that are far below where history says they should be.

But price hasn't been the only thing holding back the housing market. The Federal Reserve's senior loan officer survey shows that as the economy has recovered, banks have been far slower to relax lending standards for mortgages than for other types of loans. In recent quarters they have actually tightened a bit.

Applying hard lessons from the bust, households also may be doing some self regulation, demanding higher down payments of themselves and more comfort that they will have the means to make payments.

Meeting both of those criteria depends on a better job market. Although the Labor Department reported that U.S. payrolls finally rose past their January 2008 peak last month, the population rose by about 15 million people during that period. Moreover, adjusted for inflation, average hourly earnings have made no progress since the recession ended five years ago this month.

It is cheering that the price and supply problems that have held back housing are showing some improvement. True recovery, though, rests on employment improving to the point that banks are more willing to extend mortgages and households are more willing to take them. There is hope that with the recent pickup in payrolls, that time is no longer over the horizon. But it hasn't come yet.

License this article from Dow Jones Reprint Service[http://www.djreprints.com/link/DJRFactiva.html?FACTIVA=wjco20140625000060]

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.S. News: New-Home Sales Jump To 6-Year High Point
By Ben Leubsdorf
568 words
25 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
7
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New-home sales soared in May to their highest level in six years, the latest evidence that the U.S. housing recovery is regaining traction after an extended slowdown.

Sales of new single-family homes rose nearly 19% from April to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 504,000, a new postrecession high, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. May sales were up 16.9% from a year earlier.

May's big increase represents just a month of data and may reflect, at least in part, the report's small sample size. The 18.6% rise -- the biggest one-month jump in new-home sales since 1992 -- came with a margin of error of plus or minus 17.3 percentage points.

"The new-home sales numbers are very volatile month-to-month. When we look at the year-to-date change, it's up only 1% year-over-year," said Jed Kolko, chief economist at real-estate website Trulia. "Future months will tell us whether new-home sales really are closer to flat, or on a big upward climb."

Sales of newly built homes account for only about 10% of U.S. home-buying activity. But the rise in new-home sales the past two months reinforces other signs of strength in the housing market this spring. Existing-home sales were up a seasonally adjusted 4.9% in May after rising 1.5% in April, the National Association of Realtors said.

"The market is shaking off the slump from earlier this year," Mr. Kolko said.

There was a 4.5-month supply of new homes on the market at the end of May, down from a 5.3-month inventory at the end of April. There was a 5.6-month supply of previously owned homes on the market at the end of last month, down from a 5.7-month supply in April.

Accelerating sales could encourage builders to step up construction. Housing starts fell 6.5% in May but jumped 12.7% the prior month, according to Commerce data.

The U.S. housing market began to heal in the years following the 2007-2009 recession, with both new- and existing-home sales rising strongly from late 2011 through mid-2013. But the recovery hit a rough patch last summer as mortgage rates jumped and prices rose, then slowed further with the arrival of harsh winter weather.

New-home sales bottomed out in March, rose in April and jumped last month to a pace last seen in May 2008. May's rise was led by the Northeast, where sales surged 54.5% from April, and the West, where sales were up 34%. New-home sales rose 14.2% in the South and ticked up 1.4% in the Midwest.

Borrowing costs have come down a bit in recent months. The average interest rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage last week was 4.17%, compared with an average of 4.43% in January, according to Freddie Mac.

Price gains have slowed, too. The S&P/Case-Shiller Home Price Index for 20 major U.S. cities, also released Tuesday, rose 10.8% in April from a year earlier, down from a 12.4% yearly pace in March.

Abreast of the Market
U.S. Rally Stalls After a Record Run
By Dan Strumpf
731 words
24 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
20
English
Copyright © 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

U.S. stocks edged down Monday, as investors paused to assess the market's string of recent gains and a batch of corporate deal news.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average slipped 30 points, or 0.2%, to 16917 in midday trading. The S&P 500 index was off two points, or 0.1%, at 1961. The Nasdaq Composite Index declined a point to 4367.

The modest moves come after a recent steady grind higher in stocks. The S&P 500 posted its third straight all-time high and its 22nd record of the year on Friday, and the index is up about 6% on the year. The Dow is up about 2% this year.

The slow climb comes as trading volumes have tumbled and stocks have failed to make big moves. Monday was on track to be the 46th straight session in which the S&P 500 closed with a move of less than 1% -- the longest such stretch since 1995.

News of a handful of corporate tie-ups was driving buying and selling in select stocks, but overall trading activity was quiet, said Keith Bliss, senior vice president at brokerage Cuttone & Co. "I think people are trying to keep their powder dry," he said.

The data calendar was light, except for a report showing that existing home sales increased 4.9% in May to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.89 million, according to the National Association of Realtors. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had forecast a rate of 4.75 million for the month.

Sean McCarthy, regional chief investment officer at Wells Fargo Private Bank, said he has been encouraged by the recent string of upbeat economic data following a slowdown earlier in the year. He said he expects stocks broadly to post modest single-digit gains this year as long as the economy continues to improve and corporate earnings grow as expected.

"What the market has wanted to see is good economic data, and overall we're getting that in the second quarter," said Mr. McCarthy, whose firm manages $170 billion.

Micros Systems rose 3.4% after The Wall Street Journal reported Oracle is close to sealing a deal to buy the software company for about $5 billion. Oracle edged up 0.9%.

Integrys Energy Group surged 13% after the company agreed to be acquired by Wisconsin Energy for $5.71 billion in cash and stock. Wisconsin Energy shares fell 2.5%.

Lululemon Athletica rallied 3.7% after The Wall Street Journal reported the yoga gear maker's founder is working with bankers at Goldman Sachs to find a way to gain more influence over the company's operations.

Central Garden & Pet climbed 9.1% after Harbinger Group said it has offered to buy the company for about $505 million, or $1.1 billion including debt. Harbinger rose 1%.

European stocks slid after a report that euro-zone business activity slowed this month and as U.K. housing shares fell on the prospect of changes to mortgage rules.

The Stoxx Europe 600 index lost 0.5% to 346.31.

Markit's preliminary June purchasing managers index for the euro zone, which measures activity across both the manufacturing and services sectors, fell to 52.8 from 53.5 in May, missing expectations of 53.3. Readings above 50 signal expansion. The main weak spot was France, where activity contracted for a second straight month, and at a quickening pace.

U.K. real-estate shares dropped following reports that the Bank of England this week will propose measures aimed at paring the pace of growth in the country's booming housing market. Britain's finance minister, George Osborne, this month gave the central bank new powers to cap mortgage-lending limits, and the bank's Financial Policy Committee is expected to unveil recommendations Thursday.

British home builders Barratt Developments and Persimmon lost 2.7% and 1.1%, respectively, and Taylor Wimpey gave up 1.7%.

Alstom reversed course and fell 4.1%. France on Sunday won an option to buy a 20% stake in the company from France's Bouygues. The move was part of an agreement to support General Electric's bid to purchase Alstom's energy business.

Home Prices' Growth Slower Than Expected, Case-Shiller Index Says; Home Price Index Increased 10.8% in Year Ended in April, Below Expectations
By Kathleen Madigan
329 words
24 June 2014
05:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The yearly growth in home prices across the U.S. slowed more than expected in April, according to a home price report released Tuesday.

The home price index covering 10 major U.S. cities increased 10.8% in the year ended in April, said the S&P/Case-Shiller Home Price Index report. The 20-city price index also advanced 10.8%. That is down from a 12.4% yearly pace in March and less than the 11.4% expected by economists.

In November, the year-over-year growth for home prices in 20 major cities hit a cyclical peak of 13.7%.

"Overall, prices are rising month-to-month but at a slower rate," said David M. Blitzer, chairman of the index committee at S&PDow Jones Indices. "Last year some Sunbelt cities were seeing year-over-year numbers close to 30%, now all are below 20%."

On an unadjusted basis, the 10-city index increased 1.0% and the 20-city composite rose 1.1%. Seasonally adjusted, the 10-city gauge was unchanged, while the 20-city index rose 0.2%.

Although housing affordability remains attractive when compared historically, the rise in home prices and a 2013 spike in mortgage rates mean home buying this year is more expensive compared with last spring.

Still, home demand may be picking up after a weak winter.

"Near-term economic factors favor further gains in housing: mortgage rates are lower than a year ago, the Fed is expected to keep interest rates steady until mid-2015 and the labor market is improving," Mr. Blitzer said.

The National Association of Realtors reported Monday that sales of existing homes increased a better-than-expected 4.9% to an annual rate of 4.89 million in May, the highest sales rate since October.

U.S. News: Home Sales Jump As Market Rebounds
By Eric Morath
545 words
24 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Sales of previously owned homes rose strongly in May but remain shy of last year's pace, a sign the housing market has tentatively regained its footing this spring.

The National Association of Realtors said Monday that sales of existing homes increased 4.9% last month to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.89 million homes. That was the highest pace since October, the last month annualized sales topped the five-million mark.

Coupled with an improvement in April, the May figure suggests the housing market has bounced back from a dismal winter and buyers are adjusting to modestly higher interest rates compared with the spring of 2013.

Still, May sales were down 5% from a year earlier and the recent rebound could be slowed if interest rates on mortgages begin to rise, as many economists project. Because of the sluggish start to 2014, the Realtor group expects a 3% decline in sales this year from 2013's 5.09 million.

"It would be risky to assume this gain marks the start of a sustainable rebound," said Ian Shepherdson, chief economist at Pantheon Macroeconomics. The recent sales gains are largely "a reversal of the winter hit," and other reports do "not support the idea that demand is recovering," he said.

After a strong start last year, home sales began to ease in August before perking up this spring. The roller coaster coincides with interest rates that jumped a percentage point in the spring of 2013 but have moderated recently, touching a six-month low in May. The average interest rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage stood at 4.17% last week, according to Freddie Mac.

Lower mortgage rates make home purchases more affordable for borrowers. But NAR economist Lawrence Yun said he expects rates to start moving back up soon, reaching 5% by the first quarter of 2015.

Rates have edged lower this year even as the Federal Reserve scaled back its bond-buying program designed to push down long-term interest rates. That program is expected to end later this year, and then the Fed will contemplate increasing short-term rates from near zero.

Monday's report showed the median sale price of a home in May was up 5.1% from a year earlier to $213,400 -- the slowest annual gain since March 2012.

The inventory of homes available for sale rose 6% from a year earlier. At the current pace, it would take 5.6 months to exhaust the supply -- just below a typical level. The increased inventory drove May's strong sales pace, said IHS Global Insight U.S. economist Stephanie Karol.

A larger inventory of homes could set up a virtuous cycle where the better selection draws buyers to the market, which in turn sustains price gains. That then entices additional sellers to list. One weak link in that chain, however, could be first-time buyers. The share of first-time buyers fell in May even as sales rose.

"The market can only retain its strength for so long if move-up buyers cannot find a first-time buyer to purchase their starter homes," Ms. Karol said.
